God's Qudrah as a Separate Attribute
Sanusi proves qudrah is a separate attribute, refuting Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that God's power (qudrah) must be a separate attribute from His essence, and how he refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
A powerful being without power is inconceivable; power must be an attribute.

This lesson takes up the second major claim of Sanusi's discussion of divine power: that God's being powerful (qādriyya) is by a power (qudrah) that is additional to His essence. Sanusi first states the claim, then directly refutes the Mu'tazila who deny a separate attribute of power. Finally, he provides a logical analysis showing that qudrah must be real, using four possibilities: condition, cause, sign, or part of the essence. This passage is a classic Kalam argument for the reality of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a refutation, and a logical argument.
2
Identify the three parts
Break the passage into: (1) affirmation of the second claim, (2) refutation of Mu'tazila, (3) logical analysis of qudrah. See how Sanusi moves from assertion to proof.
3
Understand the logical disjunction
Sanusi says qudrah must be one of four things: condition, cause, sign, or part of the essence. Ask yourself why these are exhaustive. This is a classic Kalam technique.
The list is meant to cover all possible relationships between qudrah and being qādir.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Why does Sanusi say the Mu'tazila view is 'obviously corrupt'? Because a qādir without qudrah is unintelligible (lā yuʿqal). This is a rational argument, not scriptural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( بقدرةٍ ( هذا المجرورُ يتعلَّق بقوله : ( قادراً ) وهذا إثبات للمطلب الثاني؛ وهو كونُ قادريَّتِهِ تعالى بقدرة زائدة على الذات، لا كما تقوله المعتزلة مِنْ نفي القدرةِ، وقادريَّتُهُ تعالى عندهم بذاتِهِ جلَّ وعزَّ، لا بقدرة زائدة على ذاته، ولا يخفى فساد هذا المذهب؛ فإنَّ قادراً لا قدرة لهُ لا يُعقل؛ لأنَّ القدرة إمَّا شرط في كون القادر قادراً، أو علَّةٌ له، أو مدلول له، أو جزء مِنْ حقيقتِهِ؛ إذ إنَّما هي
His statement: 'by a power' (bi-qudratin) - this genitive is attached to his statement 'powerful' (qādiran). And this is an affirmation of the second claim, which is that His highness's being powerful (qādriyyatuhu) is by a power additional to the essence, not as the Mu'tazila say in denying power—for His being powerful according to them is by His essence, may He be glorified and exalted, not by a power additional to His essence. And the corruption of this doctrine is not hidden; for a powerful one without power is not intelligible (lā yuʿqal), because power is either a condition (sharṭ) in the powerful one's being powerful, or a cause (ʿilla) of it, or a sign (madlūl) of it, or a part (juzʾ) of his reality (ḥaqīqa); since it is indeed...
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage proves the second claim (al-maṭlab al-thānī) that God's qādriyya (being powerful) is by a power (qudrah) that is additional (zāʾida) to His essence (dhāt). It refutes the Mu'tazila who deny a separate attribute of power, claiming that God is qādir by His essence alone. Sanusi argues that this doctrine is obviously corrupt (fāsid) because a qādir without qudrah is unintelligible (lā yuʿqal). He then provides a logical analysis: qudrah must be either a condition (sharṭ) of being qādir, or a cause (ʿilla) of it, or a sign (madlūl) indicating it, or a part (juzʾ) of the essence (ḥaqīqa). In any case, it cannot be simply nonexistent. The conclusion (though implicit) is that qudrah is a real attribute distinct from the essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that God is powerful by His essence, with no separate attribute of power.
Correction: Sanusi argues that this is rationally impossible because the concept of 'powerful' necessarily entails power as a distinct reality, whether as condition, cause, sign, or part of the essence.

Checkpoint

A) The Qur'an explicitly says God has power.
B) A powerful being without power is unintelligible.
C) The Mu'tazila are a deviant sect.
D) Power cannot be a condition of being powerful.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi argues that God's power must be a separate attribute because the concept of a powerful being without power is rationally inconceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a and say 'Ya Qadir', do you truly feel that His power is a real, distinct attribute that makes Him able to answer you, or do you sometimes think of it as just a word? How does this lesson challenge you to deepen your certainty in God's power as a separate reality behind your requests?
?
The Mu'tazila denied separate attributes because they feared multiple eternal entities. Are there any theological compromises you have made in your own heart—perhaps to make God more 'simple' or 'accessible'—that actually diminish the depth of His attributes? What would it cost you to embrace the full rational necessity of His attributes as taught by the Ahl al-Sunna?
?
Sanusi says a powerful being without power is 'not intelligible'. Is your concept of God's power intelligible? Do you actively affirm that His power is a real attribute, or do you assume it automatically? How can you begin to make your tawḥīd more precise in your daily thoughts and worship?

Proof of God's Qadariyya through an Additional Power
Sanusi proves that God's attribute of qadariyya is through a power additional to His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that God's attribute of being capable (qadariyya) necessarily requires a separate attribute of power (qudra) additional to His essence, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
A capable being without power is inconceivable — power is essential to capability.

Sanusi now turns to the second claim in his discussion of divine attributes: that God's attribute of being capable is through a power that is additional to His essence. He contrasts this with the Mu'tazila view that God is capable by His essence alone, without a distinct attribute of power. This passage argues that the very concept of a 'capable being' logically entails having power, making the Mu'tazila position untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how Sanusi first states his claim, then refutes the Mu'tazila, then explains why power must exist.
The passage is a tight logical argument.
2
Identify the main claim and the refutation
Find where Sanusi affirms that qadariyya is through a power additional to the essence, and where he dismisses the Mu'tazila view as corrupt. Understand the two opposing positions.
3
Analyze the logical argument
Focus on the phrase 'for a capable one without power is inconceivable' and the four possible relationships between power and the capable: condition, cause, indication, or part. Consider why each relationship leads to the necessity of power.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that on all these accounts, the capable cannot be devoid of power. Ask yourself how this applies to God and why it refutes the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ قادريَّتُه تعالى بقدرةِ زائدة على ذاتِهِ ] قوله : ( بقدرةٍ ( هذا المجرورُ يتعلَّق بقوله : ( قادراً ) وهذا إثبات للمطلب الثاني ؛ وهو كونُ قادريَّتِهِ تعالى بقدرة زائدة على الذات ، لا كما تقوله المعتزلة مِنْ نفي القدرةِ ، وقادريَّتُهُ تعالى عندهم بذاتِهِ جلَّ وعزَّ ، لا بقدرة زائدة على ذاته ، ولا يخفى فساد هذا المذهب ؛ فإنَّ قادراً لا قدرة لهُ لا يُعقل ؛ لأنَّ القدرة إمَّا شرط في كون القادر قادراً ، أو علَّةٌ له ، أو مدلول له ، أو جزء مِنْ حقيقتِهِ ؛ إذ إنما هي القادرُ مَنْ له قدرة ، وعلى جميع هذه التقادير يستحيل أن يعرى القادر عن القدر
His qadariyya – exalted is He – is through a power additional to His essence.' His saying 'by a power' – this genitive attaches to His saying 'capable' – and this is an affirmation of the second claim, which is that His qadariyya – exalted is He – is through a power additional to the essence, not as the Muʿtazila say in negating power; rather, His qadariyya according to them is by His essence – may He be glorified and exalted – not by a power additional to His essence. And the corruption of this doctrine is not hidden; for a capable one without power is inconceivable, because power is either a condition for the capable being capable, or a cause for him, or an indication of him, or a part of his reality. Since indeed the capable is one who has power. And on all these accounts, it is impossible for the capable to be devoid of power.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the second claim (المطلب الثاني) that God's attribute of being capable (قادريته) is through a power (قدرة) that is additional to His essence. The commentator explains the phrase 'by a power' (بقدرة) in the text. The Muʿtazila are refuted because they deny power as an attribute, claiming God is capable by His essence alone. The passage argues that a capable being without power is inconceivable (لا يُعقل). The possible relationships between power and the capable are enumerated: power may be a condition, a cause, an indication, or a part of the reality of the capable. In all cases, the capable cannot be devoid of power. This proof is based on conceptual necessity.

جزء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila belief that God is capable by His essence without a separate attribute of power is correct.
Correction: Sanusi argues that 'capable' necessarily implies having power; without power, the term 'capable' has no meaning. Therefore, God must have an attribute of power additional to His essence.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is it impossible for a capable being to lack power?
A) Because power is the same as capability.
B) Because power is either a condition, cause, indication, or part of capability.

D) Because God has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi establishes that God's attribute of being capable requires a distinct attribute of power, because the concept of 'capable' logically entails having power, regardless of how power relates to capability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila thought God's essence alone suffices for capability. But you know you are not self-sufficient. In what area of your life do you still act as if you have independent power, forgetting that your capability is a gift from the Capable One? What will you do today to surrender that illusion?
?
Sanusi says a 'capable without power' is inconceivable. Yet you often claim to be 'able' while neglecting to draw on God's power through dua and tawakkul. When was the last time you felt truly powerless, and did you turn to the One whose power is perfect? Commit to one specific situation this week where you will prayerfully rely on His qudra instead of your own strength.

Refuting the Claim that God's Power is Identical with His Essence
Sanusi refutes the Peripatetic philosophers on the union of power and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the Peripatetic philosophers who assert that God's power is united with His essence, and why such a union is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
Union of power with essence would make the whole equal its part—an absurdity.

Sanusi responds to the Peripatetic philosophers who argue that God's power is not a separate attribute but identical with His essence. While this might seem like a subtle metaphysical point, Sanusi shows that it leads to a logical contradiction: it would require the whole to be identical to its part. This lesson unpacks that argument and its place in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim of the philosophers
Read the passage to see what the Peripatetics assert: that power is one with God's essence, just as they say about knowledge.
Note that the text gives two grammatical readings of 'غير'.
2
Follow the refutation argument
Identify the two refutations: (1) a previous general proof against union, and (2) the specific logical argument here: union entails whole = part or many = few, which is inconceivable.
Focus on the second refutation as the main point.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is effective. If power were identical to essence, then the combination of power and essence would be the same as just essence alone, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( غيرِ مُتَّحِدَةٍ بذاتِهِ ( يصح قراءة ( غير ) بالخفض نعتاً لـ ( قدرة ) ، وبالنصب على تقدير ( أعني ) ، ومعنى اتحادِ القدرة بذاتِهِ : أنْ تكونَ معَهُ شيئاً واحداً ، وهذا قد ذهب إليه المشاؤونَ مِنَ الفلاسفة ، كما ذهبوا إلى مثل ذلك في العلم ، ويُرَدُّ عليهم بما سبق في برهان استحالةِ اتِّحادِهِ تعالى بغيرِهِ ، وردَّ عليهم هنا في أصل العقيدة باختصار ؛ بأنَّهُ يلزمُ في الاتحادِ أنْ يكونَ الكلُّ عينَ جزئه ، أو الكثيرُ عينَ القليل ، وذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ ، وإلى هذا أشرنا
His saying: 'not united with His essence' – it is permissible to read 'non- (غير)' in genitive as an adjective for 'power', and in nominative on the assumption of 'I mean' – and the meaning of the union of power with His essence is that it be with Him one thing. And this is what the Peripatetics among the philosophers held, as they similarly held regarding knowledge. And they are refuted by what preceded in the proof of the impossibility of His – exalted – union with another. And he refutes them here in the foundation of creed briefly: that union necessitates that the whole be identical to its part, or the many be identical to the few, and that is inconceivable. And to this we have alluded.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Peripatetic philosophers who claim that God's power is identical (متحد) with His essence, i.e., they are one single thing. The text notes that the phrase 'غير متحدة بذاته' (not united with His essence) can be read as an adjective or with an implied 'I mean'. The philosophers' position is stated: power is one with the essence, as they also claim for knowledge. The refutation is given in two ways: (1) a previous proof on the impossibility of God being united with anything else, and (2) a brief argument specific to this context: union would entail that the whole (the combined entity) is identical to its part (the essence or power individually), or the many is identical to the few, which is absurd. This is a logical reductio ad absurdum showing the incoherence of identity relations between God and His attributes.

Misconception: Some might think that if power is identical to essence, then it is not a separate attribute and that is fine.
Correction: Sanusi shows that identity leads to logical absurdity: whole = part or many = few, which is inconceivable.

Checkpoint
What is the brief argument given against the philosophers' claim of union between power and essence?
A) It leads to anthropomorphism.
B) It implies that the whole is the same as its part, which is inconceivable.
C) It contradicts the Qur'an.
D) It was already refuted by al-Ashʿarī.

Key Takeaway
The Peripatetic claim that God's power is identical with His essence is refuted because it leads to the absurd conclusion that the whole equals its part.

?

Definition of Qadir and the Ontology of Qadiriyya
Sanusi defines qadir and clarifies the ontology of qadiriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of qadir (the Powerful) and the different views on the ontological status of qadiriyya (the state of being powerful) within Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Qadiriyya is not a separate attribute but the very fact of having power.

Sanusi begins by defining the powerful one (al-qadir) simply as one who possesses power (qudra). This seems straightforward, but it raises a deeper question: what exactly is qadiriyya? Is it an additional stable state subsisting in the essence, as the theory of states (ahwal) holds, or is it merely the presence of power? This passage introduces both views, preparing the ground for later proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the first line: 'al-qadir is one who has power' and note the impossibility of a powerful being being without power.
This is the basic starting point.
2
Understand the two views
Identify the two views on qadiriyya: one if states are affirmed, another if states are denied.
The second view is attributed to 'the Shaykh'.
3
Grasp the consequence
Realize that if states are denied, proving God is qadir is the same as proving He has power.
This simplifies the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القادرُ مَنْ له قدرة، وعلى جميع هذه التقادير يستحيل أن يعرى القادر عن القدر هذا كلُّهُ إنْ قلنا بثبوتِ الأحوالِ، وأنَّ القادرية حال ثابتةٌ تقومُ بالذات، وأما إن قلنا بنفي الأحوال - كما هو مذهب الشيخ ـ فلا معنى للقادرية إلا قيام القدرة بالمحل؛ فبرهانُ إثباتِ كونه تعالى قادراً هو بعينه برهانُ إثباتِ القدرةِ لهُ تعالى. قوله: ( غيرِ مُتَّحِدَةٍ بذاتِهِ ( يصح قراءة ( غير ) بالخفض نعتاً لـ ( قدرة )، وبالنصب على تقدير ( أعني )، ومعنى اتحادِ القدرة بذاتِهِ : أنْ تكونَ معَهُ شيئاً واحداً، وهذا قد ذهب إليه المشاؤونَ مِنَ الفلاسفة، كما ذهبوا إلى مثل ذلك في العلم، ويُرَدُّ عليهم بما سبق في برهان استحالةِ اتِّحادِهِ تعالى بغيرِهِ، وردَّ عليهم هنا في أصل العقيدة باختصار؛ بأنَّهُ يلزمُ في الاتحادِ أنْ يكونَ الكلُّ عينَ جزئه، أو الكثيرُ عينَ القليل، وذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ، وإلى هذا أشرنا بقولنا: ( لزمَ كونُ الاثنين واحداً ) يعني: لأنَّ القدرة والذاتَ حقيقتانِ اثنتان، فلو اتَّحدتا ـ أي: صارتا واحداً ـ لزم ما ذكر ضرورةً.
The powerful (al-qadir) is the one who has power (qudra). And according to all these considerations, it is impossible for the powerful to be devoid of power. All of this is if we affirm the states (ahwal), and that qadiriyya (the state of being powerful) is a stable state subsisting in the essence. But if we deny the states—as is the doctrine of the Shaykh—then there is no meaning for qadiriyya except the subsistence of power in the subject. Thus the proof for establishing that He, exalted, is powerful is the very same proof for establishing power for Him, exalted. His statement: 'not united with His essence'—it is permissible to read 'not' (ghayr) in the genitive as an adjective for 'power', and in the accusative with the implied meaning 'I mean'. The meaning of the union (ittihad) of power with His essence is that it be with Him one single thing. This has been held by the Peripatetics (al-masha'un) among the philosophers, just as they held the like of that concerning knowledge. They are refuted by what preceded in the proof of the impossibility of His, exalted, union with other than Him, and He refuted them here in the foundation of the creed briefly: that in union it would necessitate that the whole be the same as its part, or the many be the same as the few, and that is not intelligible. And to this we alluded by our saying: 'The two would become one', meaning: because power and essence are two realities, so if they unite—i.e., become one—what was mentioned would necessarily follow.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage defines 'qadir' (the powerful) and then discusses the nature of 'qadiriyya' (the state of being powerful) in relation to the theory of states (ahwal). It then refutes the philosophers' view that power is identical with God's essence. The passage begins by stating that the powerful one is the one who has power, and it is impossible for the powerful to be devoid of power. This is based on the view that states exist as stable attributes. Then it considers the alternative view that denies states, in which case qadiriyya simply means the existence of power in the subject, so the proof for God being qadir is the same as the proof for His having power. Then it addresses the statement 'not united with His essence' and explains that it can be read as an adjective or as an explicit mention. The meaning of union of power with essence is that they are one thing, which is the view of the Peripatetic philosophers. They are refuted by the earlier proof of the impossibility of union with anything else, and specifically here by the argument that union would require the whole to be identical with its part, or the many with the few, which is unintelligible. This is alluded to by the phrase 'the two would become one', because power and essence are two distinct realities; if united, the absurdity follows.

قدرة
قادرية
حال

Checkpoint

A) The power itself
B) A stable state subsisting in the essence
C) The mere presence of power
D) An action of God

Key Takeaway
Qadiriyya is not an additional attribute; it is the very fact that the subject possesses power, regardless of whether one accepts the theory of states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If proving God is powerful is exactly the same as proving He has power, what does that say about the unity of His attributes? Is there any area of your life where you treat God's attributes as separate from His being, rather than as expressions of His one essence?
?
The passage contrasts two theological frameworks (affirming vs. denying states). Which framework resonates more with your heart, and why? How does your view affect the way you rely on God's power in your daily struggles?

Refutation of Union of Power with Essence
Sanusi refutes the Peripatetic view that power is united with God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the Peripatetic philosophers' claim that God's power is united with His essence.

Core Idea
If power were united with God's essence, the whole would be the part—an absurdity.

Sanusi now addresses a specific heretical view: the Peripatetic philosophers held that God's power is not distinct from His essence but is one and the same. This lesson presents Sanusi's brief refutation, which draws on a general proof against union with anything else and a specific argument that union would lead to an absurd consequence—the whole being identical to its part.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' claim
Read that the Peripatetics hold power and essence to be one single thing.
This is the view being refuted.
2
Note the grammatical readings
Observe the two possible readings of 'غير متحدة بذاته'—as an adjective or as 'I mean'.
This affects interpretation but not the core argument.
3
Follow the refutation
Identify the general proof from earlier (impossibility of union with other) and the specific logical argument: union leads to whole = part.
The specific argument is the focus here.

Source Text

First Proof for the Eternity of God's Power
First proof for the eternity of God's power via reductio

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's first proof that God's power must be eternal, based on the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal and the observed existence of the world.

Core Idea
If God's power were temporal, its opposite (incapacity) would be eternal, making the world impossible.

Sanusi now moves to establish the third required point about God's power: that it is eternal. He offers two proofs; this lesson covers the first. The proof is a reductio ad absurdum: if power were temporal, then incapacity would be eternal, which contradicts the observed existence of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces the proof and its logical structure.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the key Arabic terms: قدرة (power), قدم (eternity), حادثة (temporal), عجز (incapacity), قديم (eternal). Understand their meanings in context.
These terms are foundational for the proof.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the argument step by step: if power is temporal → incapacity is eternal → incapacity cannot cease to exist → power cannot come to exist → world cannot exist → but world does exist → contradiction.
This is a classic reductio argument.
4
Connect to earlier principle
Recall the principle that the eternal cannot cease to exist, established earlier in the section on the world's origination. Sanusi cites this to show that eternal incapacity cannot be removed.
5
Understand the conclusion
The proof concludes that power cannot be temporal; therefore, it must be eternal. Reflect on why this is necessary for God's attribute of power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( قديمةٍ ( تُقرَأُ بالخفض نعتاً لـ ( قدرة )، وأشار بهذا إلى إثبات المطلب الثالث؛ وهو قدم القدرةِ؛ أي : لا أَوَّلَ لها، واستدل على ذلك بدليلين. الأوّلُ : أنَّ القدرة لو كانَتْ حادثة لكان ضدُّها ـ وهو العجز - قديماً؛ إذْ لا واسطة بينهما في حقٌّ كلِّ حيٌّ، وإذا كانَ العجز قديماً استحالَ عدمُهُ؛ لما عرفتَ في بابِ حدوثِ العالمِ مِنْ بيانِ استحالة عدم القديم، وإذا استحال عدم العجز استحال وجود القدرة التي هي شرط في وجودِ العالم، فيلزم ألا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ منه أبداً، والعِيانُ يُكذِّبه
His statement: 'eternal (qadima)'—it is read in the genitive as an adjective for 'power' (qudra). And by this he referred to establishing the third required point, which is the eternity (qidam) of power, i.e., it has no beginning. And he adduced for that two proofs. The first: that if power were temporal (haditha), then its opposite—incapacity (ajz)—would be eternal (qadim), since there is no intermediary between them in the case of every living being. And if incapacity is eternal, its non-existence is impossible, because of what you have known in the section on the origination of the world concerning the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal. And if the non-existence of incapacity is impossible, then the existence of power, which is a condition for the existence of the world, is impossible. Thus it follows that nothing of it would ever exist, but direct observation (al-iyan) belies that.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the eternity of God's power, which is the third required point (after establishing that God has power and that His power is not identical with His essence). The proof begins by introducing the statement 'eternal' as an adjective for power. Then the first proof: if power were temporal (haditha), its opposite, incapacity (ajz), would be eternal (qadim), because for every living being there is no middle state between power and incapacity. If incapacity is eternal, its non-existence is impossible, as established earlier in the discussion of the world's origination that the eternal cannot cease to exist. If incapacity cannot be removed, then power—which is necessary for the existence of the world—cannot come into existence. This would mean that the world never comes into existence. But we observe the world exists, so this is false. Therefore, power cannot be temporal; it must be eternal.

Key Terms
قدرة
قدم
حادثة
عجز
قديم
استحالة عدم القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if incapacity is eternal, it does not affect power because God could still have power.
Correction: The proof shows that if incapacity is eternal, it cannot be removed, so power (its opposite) cannot come into existence. Thus, eternal incapacity prevents power from existing at all.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if God's power were temporal according to the first proof?
A) Incapacity would be eternal, preventing the world's existence.
B) Power would be infinite.
C) God would be unable to create.
D) The world would still exist.

Key Takeaway
God's power must be eternal because if it were temporal, incapacity would be eternal, making the world's existence impossible, which contradicts observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power were not eternal, your very existence and every moment of your life would be contingent on a power that might not have been there. Does this thought press you to reflect on the certainty of your reliance on an eternally powerful Creator? What would change in your daily life if you truly felt this dependency?
?
You have just read a proof that God's power cannot have a beginning. Yet do you live as though His power is always present and sufficient for every trial you face? In what specific area of your life are you acting—by worry or control—as if God's power might have a 'gap' or a beginning?
?
The proof uses observation of the world as evidence for God's eternal power. When you look at the world around you—your own heartbeat, the sun rising, the order of the universe—are you moved to recognize the eternal power behind it, or do you take it for granted? What would it take for you to see each moment as a direct testimony to God's eternal power?

Second Proof for Eternity of God's Power
Sanusi argues that a temporal divine power leads to circularity or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if God's power were temporal, it would necessitate either circularity or infinite regress, both of which are impossible, thereby proving His power is eternal.

Core Idea
If God's power were temporal, it would lead to an impossible circularity or infinite regress.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a second proof for the eternity of God's power. He argues that a temporal power would depend on a maker with power, leading to a regress that either loops back on itself or continues infinitely. Since both are impossible, the original power must be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: قدرة (power), حادثة (temporal), دور (circularity), تسلسل (infinite regress), مماثلة (similarity).
2
Identify the assumption and dilemma
Note that the argument starts with the assumption that power is temporal (حادثة). From this, two possibilities arise: circularity or infinite regress.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: temporal power needs a maker with power; that maker's power, being similar, would also be temporal; then it depends on another power; if that is the first, circularity; otherwise, the same reasoning applies, leading to regression.
The argument uses the principle of similarity (مماثلة) to extend temporality.
4
Understand impossibility of both outcomes
Circularity (دور) is impossible because it would mean a thing depends on itself. Infinite regress (تسلسل) is impossible because it would require an endless chain of causes, which is rationally unacceptable.
These are standard kalam principles: both دور and تسلسل are impossible.
5
Conclude the necessity of eternal power
Since the assumption of temporal power leads to an impossibility, the assumption must be false. Therefore, God's power is eternal (قديم).
This is a proof by contradiction (برهان الخلف).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنَّ القدرة لو كانَتْ حادثة للزمَ الدَّورُ أو التسلسل، وبيانُ : أنَّها إذا كانَتْ حادثةٌ لزمَ افتقارها إلى مُحدِثِ قادرِ بقدرةٍ، ثم اللزومِ ننقل الكلام أيضاً إلى هذهِ القدرةِ التي توقفت عليها القدرة الأولى فيلزم أن تكونَ أيضاً حادثةً؛ لمماثلتها للأولى، فتتوقَّفُ هي أيضاً على قدرة أخرى للفاعل؛ فإنْ كانَتْ هذه الأخرى هي الأولى التي كانَتْ توقفت عليها لزمَ الدَّورُ، وإنْ كانَتْ غيرها لزم فيها أيضاً ما لزم
The second: that if power were temporal, then circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would necessarily follow. And the explanation: that if it is temporal, it must depend on a maker (muhdith) who is powerful by a power. Then, due to this necessity, we shift the discussion also to this power upon which the first power depended, so it would be necessary that it also be temporal, due to its similarity to the first. Thus it also depends on another power of the agent. If this other power is the first one upon which it depended, then circularity occurs; and if it is other than it, then what was necessary also applies to it.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the eternity of God's power. It argues that if power were temporal (haditha), it would require a maker who himself has power. That maker's power would also have to be temporal, since it is similar to the first. That power would then depend on another power of the agent. If that other power is the first power, circularity (dawr) results; if it is a different power, the same reasoning applies, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Both circularity and infinite regress are impossible. Therefore, power must be eternal. The argument is based on the impossibility of a regress of causes and the impossibility of circular dependence.

Key Terms
قدرة
حادثة
دور
تسلسل
مماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a temporal power could be caused by something that itself does not possess power, breaking the chain.
Correction: The proof argues that the maker of a temporal power must be powerful (قادر بقدرة), because producing an effect requires power. The maker's power, being similar, would also be temporal, leading to the same need.

Checkpoint
What two logical consequences arise if God's power were temporal according to the second proof?
A) Power would be eternal or non-existent.
B) Circularity or infinite regress.
C) Power would be simple or composite.
D) Incapacity or weakness.

Key Takeaway
God's power must be eternal because any temporal power would lead to an impossible circularity or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power were not eternal, it would depend on something else. How does the certainty that God's power is uncaused and eternal change the way you trust Him in your hardest moments? Will you consciously lean on that truth today?
?
The proof shows that circularity and infinite regress are impossible in God's attributes. When you find yourself doubting God's perfect attributes—like His power or knowledge—are you unconsciously falling into a similar logical trap? What step of Sanusi's argument do you need to hold onto most?
?
Sanusi's proof demands that God's power be eternal, not borrowed or dependent. In what area of your life have you been acting as if God's power needs your help or something else to be effective? What would it look like to surrender that to His eternal power starting now?

Why Power and Essence Cannot Be One
Sanusi explains the impossibility of power and essence being one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that divine power and essence cannot be identical, based on their being two distinct realities.

Core Idea
Two distinct realities cannot become one without contradiction.

Sanusi continues his defense of the distinction between divine attributes and essence. Here he explains a crucial point: if power were identical to essence, then the two would have to become one — but they are already two distinct realities. This short passage unpacks the logical necessity behind that impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noting the key terms 'القدرة' (power) and 'الذات' (essence). Then read the English translation.
The passage is a single sentence, so read it carefully.
2
Identify the two realities
Sanusi states that power and essence are 'two realities' (حقيقتان اثنتان). Recognize that each is a distinct entity in Islamic theology.
This distinction is fundamental to Ash'ari attribute theology.
3
Understand the conditional
The passage presents a conditional: 'if they were united... what was mentioned would necessarily follow.' Think about why union of two distinct things would lead to that consequence.
The consequence was discussed earlier in the text.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this matters: if power and essence were identical, then either power would be unnecessary (since essence suffices) or essence would be multiple. This preserves the distinctness of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقولنا: (لزم كون الاثنين واحداً) يعني: لأن القدرة والذات حقيقتان اثنتان، فلو اتحدتا ـ أي صارتا واحداً ـ لزم ما ذكر ضرورةً.
By our saying: (it would necessitate the two being one) means: because power and essence are two realities, so if they were united — i.e., became one — what was mentioned would necessarily follow.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a previous statement. Sanusi clarifies that the phrase 'it would necessitate the two being one' refers to the fact that power and essence are two distinct realities. If they were to become one (unite), then the previously mentioned consequence would necessarily result. The passage guards against mistakenly thinking that the attribute (power) is identical to the essence without distinction.

Two Proofs for the Eternity of Divine Power
Sanusi gives two proofs for the eternity of divine power and clarifies the scope of 'tasalsul'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs for the eternity of divine power and the relationship between circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul).

Core Idea
If divine power were temporal, the world would never exist—observation disproves that.

Sanusi now tackles the third demand in his creed: proving that divine power is eternal. He offers two concise but powerful arguments. The first draws on the impossibility of eternal incapacity, while the second shows that temporality would lead to either circularity or infinite regress. He also clarifies why he mentions only 'tasalsul' in his text, ensuring you don't misunderstand his brevity.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a brief statement, then two proofs, then a methodological note.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Analyze the first proof
Identify the logical chain: if power were temporal, then incapacity (its opposite) would be eternal. Since eternal incapacity cannot cease, power (a condition for the world) cannot exist—contradicting observed existence.
This proof relies on the impossibility of the nonexistence of the eternal.
3
Analyze the second proof
Trace the regress: temporal power needs a cause with power; that cause's power is similar and thus also temporal, requiring another power. If it loops back, that's circularity; if it continues indefinitely, that's infinite regress. Both are impossible.
Sanusi had earlier established the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress.
4
Understand the tasalsul explanation
Sanusi notes he only mentioned 'tasalsul' (infinite regress) in his creed, but this includes 'dawr' (circularity) because circularity is a finite regress. This prevents misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (قديمةٍ) تُقرأ بالخفض نعتاً لـ (قدرة)، وأشار بهذا إلى إثبات المطلب الثالث؛ وهو قدم القدرة؛ أي: لا أول لها، واستدل على ذلك بدليلين:
الأول: أن القدرة لو كانت حادثة لكان ضدها ـ وهو العجز ـ قديماً؛ إذ لا واسطة بينهما في حق كل حي، وإذا كان العجز قديماً استحال عدمه؛ لما عرفت في باب حدوث العالم من بيان استحالة عدم القديم، وإذا استحال عدم العجز استحال وجود القدرة التي هي شرط في وجود العالم، فيلزم ألا يوجد شيء منه أبداً، والعيان يكذبه.
الثاني: أن القدرة لو كانت حادثة للزم الدور أو التسلسل، وبيان: أنها إذا كانت حادثة لزم افتقارها إلى محدث قادر بقدرة، ثم ننقل الكلام أيضاً إلى هذه القدرة التي توقفت عليها القدرة الأولى فيلزم أن تكون أيضاً حادثة؛ لمماثلتها للأولى، فتتوقف هي أيضاً على قدرة أخرى للفاعل؛ فإن كانت هذه الأخرى هي الأولى التي كانت توقفت عليها لزم الدور، وإن كانت غيرها لزم فيها أيضاً ما لزم في الأولى، وهكذا أبداً، ولزم التسلسل، وقد علمت استحالة الدور والتسلسل في باب وجوب قدمه تعالى ووجوب بقائه. وإنما اقتصر هنا في العقيدة على التسلسل؛ لأنه أخذه بالمعنى الأعم الشامل للدور، ولأن الدور تسلسل أيضاً، لكن في أمور متناهية، ولهذا كثيراً ما يقتصر بعض العلماء على التسلسل في باب القدم وغيره مما يلزم فيه الدور أو التسلسل بالمعنى الأخص؛ وهو الذي يكون في أمور غير متناهية، فيتوهم القاصر نقصاً في كلامهم، وليس فيه نقص؛ لما عرفت الآن في معنى التسلسل الأعم، فتنبه لذلك.
His saying: (eternal) is read with kasra as an attribute of (power), and by this he indicated the proof of the third demand: which is the eternity of power; i.e., it has no beginning, and he adduced for that two proofs: The first: that if power were temporal, its opposite — which is incapacity — would be eternal; since there is no intermediate between them in the case of every living being, and if incapacity were eternal its nonexistence would be impossible, based on what you learned in the chapter on the temporal origination of the world concerning the impossibility of the nonexistence of the eternal, and if the nonexistence of incapacity is impossible then the existence of power, which is a condition for the existence of the world, is impossible, so it would follow that nothing of it ever exists, but direct observation falsifies it. The second: that if power were temporal, circularity or infinite regress would be necessary, and its explanation: that if it were temporal, it would depend on an efficient cause capable with power, then we also transfer the discussion to this power upon which the first power depended, so it would also have to be temporal due to its similarity to the first, so it also depends on another power of the agent; if this other is the first upon which it depended, then circularity would follow, and if it is other than it, then what applied to the first also applies to it, and so on forever, and infinite regress would follow, and you have learned the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress in the chapter on the necessity of His eternality and the necessity of His perdurance. And he only confined himself here in the creed to infinite regress because he took it in the more general sense including circularity, and because circularity is also an infinite regress, but in finite matters, and for this reason many scholars often confine themselves to infinite regress in the chapter of eternality and other matters which involve circularity or infinite regress in the more specific sense — which is in non-finite matters, so the deficient person might imagine a deficiency in their speech, but there is no deficiency because of what you now know about the meaning of the more general infinite regress, so be attentive to that.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a proof and methodological clarification. Sanusi first states that the word 'eternal' (قديمة) is an attribute of 'power' (قدرة). He then claims the third demand: the eternity of divine power, meaning it has no beginning. He provides two proofs. The first proof: if power were temporal (haditha), then its opposite, incapacity (al-ajz), would be eternal. This is because there is no middle state between power and incapacity in any living being. If incapacity were eternal, its nonexistence would be impossible (as established in the proof of the world's temporality). Thus, the existence of power (which is a condition for the world's existence) would be impossible, and the world would never exist—but observation disproves this. So power cannot be temporal. The second proof: if power were temporal, it would depend on an agent capable with power. That agent's power would also be temporal (since similar to the first), requiring another power, leading to either circularity (if it is the same power) or infinite regress (if different). Both are impossible as previously proven. Sanusi then notes that in his creed he only mentioned infinite regress (tasalsul) because he took it in the general sense that includes circularity (dawr). He explains that circularity is a form of infinite regress but in finite matters, so many scholars use 'tasalsul' alone in such contexts to avoid redundancy. He warns against the mistake of thinking their speech is deficient.

Key Terms
قدم القدرة
حادثة
عجز
دور
تسلسل
العيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that mentioning only 'tasalsul' (infinite regress) in the creed is insufficient because circularity (dawr) is not explicitly mentioned.
Correction: Sanusi explains that 'tasalsul' is used here in a general sense that includes circularity, as circularity is a form of finite regress.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, if power were temporal, what would its opposite (al-ajz) be?
A) Temporal
B) Eternal
C) Nonexistent
D) Contradictory

Key Takeaway
Divine power must be eternal because its temporality would lead to impossible consequences—either the non-existence of the world or a vicious regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power is eternal and never weakens, why do you sometimes act as if He cannot change your situation? Which specific excuse are you clinging to that denies His eternal power?
?
The proof shows that if God's power were temporal, the world would never exist. How does this truth confront your impatience when you feel God's help is delayed?
?
You believe God's power is eternal. But do you live as if His power is limited by your circumstances? What would change if you truly internalized that His power has no beginning and no end?

The Scope of Divine Power: Only the Possible
Sanusi enumerates the three categories and explains exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power (al-qudra) relates only to possible things and not to the necessary or impossible.

Core Idea
God's power is limited to the realm of possibility—not necessity or impossibility.

Sanusi explains a crucial distinction in Islamic theology: divine power has a specific scope. It can only bring about possible things, not change the nature of the necessary or make the impossible existent. This passage clarifies the boundaries of God's omnipotence within rational theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the three categories: necessary, possible, impossible.
The text uses the term 'الجائزات' as a synonym for possible.
2
Identify the reasons for exclusion
Focus on why power does not relate to the necessary (would imply it came after non-existence) and to the impossible (would make it valid).
These reasons are based on logical necessity.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What does this say about God's power? Does it limit God? No, it defines the rational object of power.
This distinction preserves the necessary nature of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويلزم أن تكون هذه القدرة متعلقة بجميع الممكنات) يعني بالممكنات: الجائزات، دون الواجب والمستحيل.
أما الواجب: فكذاته تعالى وصفاته، وإنما لم تتعلق بالواجب؛ لما يلزم من تعلقها به أن يكون موجوداً بعد عدم؛ لأن ذلك من لازم أثر القدرة، وذلك قلب لحقيقة الواجب؛ إذ هو الذي لا يتصور في العقل عدمه.
وأما المستحيل: فكالجمع بين وجود الشيء وعدمه مثلاً، وإنما لم تتعلق به؛ لما يلزم في تعلقها به أن يصح وجوده، وذلك أيضاً
His saying: (And it is necessary that this power be related to all possible things) means by possible things: contingent things, excluding the necessary and the impossible. As for the necessary: like His essence and His attributes, and it is not related to the necessary because its relation to it would entail its existing after nonexistence; since that is from the requisite of the effect of power, and that would overturn the reality of the necessary, for it is that whose nonexistence is not conceivable in the mind. As for the impossible: like the combination of existence and nonexistence of a thing, for example, and it is not related to it because its relation to it would entail that its existence be valid, and that also...
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation. Sanusi states that divine power (al-qudra) must relate to all possible things (al-mumkinat), meaning contingent existents (al-ja'izat), not to the necessary (al-wajib) or the impossible (al-mustahil). He explains: the necessary includes God's essence and attributes. Power does not relate to them because that would imply that the necessary came into existence after nonexistence, which is a characteristic of the effect of power; this would contradict the nature of the necessary, which cannot be conceived as nonexistent. As for the impossible, such as combining existence and nonexistence, power does not relate to it because that would make the impossible possible, which is contradictory. The passage ends abruptly, but the point is that divine power is limited in its objects only by the nature of possibility.

Key Terms
الممكنات
الجائزات
الواجب
المستحيل
تعلق القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God's power can do anything, including making the necessary nonexistent or the impossible existent.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that power only relates to possible things; the necessary is already eternal and the impossible is logically incoherent.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is impossible
B) Because it would require the necessary to be after non-existence
C) Because God does not want to
D) Because it is not a possible thing

Key Takeaway
Divine power is confined to possible things; it cannot affect the necessary or impossible by definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What does this teach you about the importance of using the means He has established (like repentance) rather than hoping for a miracle that contradicts His order?
?

You want the reward of obedience without giving up the pleasure of sin. You are asking for an impossibility. Will you today choose to stop demanding the impossible from God and instead submit to what is possible: sincere repentance and struggle?
?
If God's power cannot change what is necessary (His essence and attributes), then every attribute of God is fixed and reliable. How does this confront your moments of doubt—when you feel God might not be powerful enough to help you? Does your prayer reflect a belief in a God whose power is limited to the possible, or do you secretly doubt that He can handle your situation? What step will you take this week to trust in His power over the possible?

6 min
The Scope of Divine Power: Only Possible Things
Sanusi defines the objects of divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power relates only to possible things, and why this does not indicate any deficiency in His power.

Core Idea
God's power is perfect because it only acts on what is genuinely possible.

Sanusi begins by clarifying the scope of divine power. Power, by definition, brings things from non-existence to existence. Therefore, it cannot relate to the necessary (which cannot not exist) nor the impossible (which cannot exist). This lesson explores that foundational distinction and addresses a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on objects of power
Read the Arabic passage twice: first to get the flow, then to identify the three categories of objects mentioned: necessary, impossible, and possible.
Notice how Sanusi defines each category before giving the reason for exclusion or inclusion.
2
Understand why the necessary is excluded
Focus on the explanation: if power related to the necessary, it would imply that the necessary came into existence after non-existence, which contradicts its necessity.
The necessary is that whose non-existence is inconceivable.
3
Understand why the impossible is excluded
Similarly, if power related to the impossible, it would imply that the impossible can exist, which contradicts its impossibility.
The impossible is that whose existence is inconceivable.
4
Grasp the definition of the possible
The possible is what the mind can conceive as both existing and non-existing. This is the proper object of power.
5
Address the misconception of deficiency
Sanusi then explains that the exclusion of the necessary and impossible is not a deficiency in power because deficiency only occurs if a thing is suitable for being an effect but power fails to affect it. Here they are not suitable.
This guards against the wrong belief that God's power is limited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عموم تعلق قدرته تعالى بكل ممكن ]
قوله : ( ويلزم أن تكون هذه القدرة مُتعلقة بجميع الممكنات ) يعني بالممكنات : الجائزات ، دون الواجب والمستحيل .
أما الواجبُ : فكذاتِهِ تعالى وصفاتِه ، وإنَّما لم تتعلق بالواجب ؛ لما يلزمُ مِنْ تعلقها بهِ أنْ يكونَ موجوداً بعد عدم ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ مِنْ لازم أثر القدرة ، وذلكَ قلب لحقيقة الواجب ؛ إذْ هو الذي لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمه
وأما المستحيل : فكالجمع بين وجودِ الشيء وعدمه مثلاً ، وإنَّما لم تتعلق بهِ ؛ لما يلزم في تعلقها به أن يصح وجوده ، وذلك أيضاً (۱) تقدم ( ص ۹۲ ) .
منافٍ لحقيقة المستحيل ؛ إذْ هو ما لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده فتعيَّنَ : أنَّ القدرة لا تتعلَّقُ إلا بالجائز ؛ وهو الممكن ؛ لأنَّهُ الذي يصحُ في العقلِ وجودُهُ وعدمُهُ ، ولا يُتوهّمُ أَنَّ في عدم تعلُّق القدرة بالواجب والمستحيل قصوراً ؛ لأنَّ القصورَ إِنَّما يثبتُ أَنْ لو كانَتْ حقيقة الشيء مما يقبلُ الوجود بعد العدم ، ثم معَ ذلك لم يتأتَ أنْ يكون أثراً للقدرةِ ، أمَّا إذا كانَتْ حقيقةُ الشيء خارجةً عن جنسِ المقدور .. فليسَ في عدم تعلَّقِ القدرةِ بهِ قصور ألبتة ، بل في تعلُّقها به يلزم قصورها ، بل عدمُها ألبتة ؛ لأنَّها لو تعلَّقتْ بالواجب كالذاتِ العلية مثلاً .. لزم حدوث الذاتِ ، وحدوثها يمنع وجود القدرة القائمة بها قبلها (۱) وقوله : ( بجميعِ الممكناتِ ( أشار بهذا إلى إثباتِ المطلبِ الرابع ؛ وهو تعميم القدرة في جميع الممكنات ؛ ردّاً لمذهب المعتزلة الذين أخرجوا أفعال العبادِ الاختياريَّةَ عن أن تكون مقدورةً الله تعالى وجعلوا العبادَ هم الذين اخترعوها بإرادتهم !
واحتج في العقيدة لمذهب أهل الحقِّ : بأنَّ القدرة لو تعلَّقَتْ ببعض الممكناتِ دونَ بعض .. للزمَ عليهِ إمَّا حدوثها ، أو انقلاب الممكنِ مستحيلاً ، وبيانُ اللزوم : أنَّ الممكناتِ متماثلة في الإمكان المصححِ
(1) إنما لزم حدوثها لأن تعلق القدرة تعلق تأثير ، ولا معنى لتعلق القدرة بالواجب إلا إيجاده وإمداده بأسباب البقاء ، وكل منهما يستلزم الحدوث . « صفاقسي
))
٢٧٤
. ( ٢٧٢/٢ )
Generality of His power's relation to every possible. He said: 'And it is necessary that this power be related to all possible things.' He means by possible things: the permissible, not the necessary nor the impossible. As for the necessary: such as His essence and His attributes. It is not related to the necessary because its relation to it would require that it exist after non-existence since that is a necessary consequence of the effect of power, and that would overturn the reality of the necessary, for it is that which cannot be conceived in the mind as non-existent. As for the impossible: such as combining existence and non-existence of a thing, for example. It is not related to it because its relation to it would require that it be sound for it to exist, and that too contradicts the reality of the impossible, for it is that whose existence cannot be conceived in the mind. So it is determined that power is only related to the permissible, which is the possible, because it is that whose existence and non-existence are sound in the mind. And it should not be imagined that in the non-relation of power to the necessary and the impossible there is deficiency; because deficiency is only established if the thing's reality were of the kind that accepts existence after non-existence, and then despite that it could not be an effect of power. But if the thing's reality is outside the genus of the powerful over, then there is no deficiency whatsoever in the non-relation of power to it. Rather, in its relation to it there would be a deficiency in power, or rather its non-existence altogether; because if it related to the necessary like the exalted essence, for example, the origination of the essence would be necessary, and its origination would prevent the existence of the power subsisting in it before it. And his saying: 'with all possible things' pointed by this to establishing the fourth point, which is the generality of power over all possible things, in refutation of the doctrine of the Mu'tazila who exclude the voluntary actions of servants from being powerful over by God Almighty, and they make the servants themselves the ones who invent them by their own will! And he argued in the creed for the doctrine of the people of truth: that if power were related to some possible things without others, there would follow from it either its origination or the conversion of the possible into impossible. And the explanation of the entailment: that possible things are similar in the possibility that renders them suitable (for being objects of power). (Footnote: It entailed origination only because the relation of power is a relation of effect; and there is no meaning to the relation of power to the necessary except bringing it into existence and supplying it with means of permanence, each of which necessitates origination. — Safaqisi)
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a detailed doctrinal argument about the universality of God's power (qudra). It begins by defining the objects of power: God's power relates only to possible things (mumkinat), which are the permissible (ja'izat), and not to the necessary (wajib) or impossible (mustahil). The author explains why: if power related to the necessary, it would imply that the necessary came into existence after non-existence, which contradicts its necessity. Similarly, relating to the impossible would require that the impossible be existent, which contradicts its impossibility. Therefore, power is confined to the possible, defined as what the mind can conceive as both existing and non-existing. The author then addresses a potential misunderstanding: that restricting power to the possible might seem like a deficiency. He argues that deficiency only occurs if a thing's nature makes it suitable for being an effect of power, but it is not. If a thing's reality is beyond the class of what can be acted upon, then not relating to it is not a deficiency; rather, relating to it would imply a deficiency or even non-existence of power. For example, if power related to the necessary, it would necessitate the origination of the essence, which would nullify the power subsisting in it. Next, the author turns to the fourth point, which is the universality of power over all possible things, in refutation of the Mu'tazilites who claimed that human voluntary actions are not within God's power but are created by humans themselves. He provides a proof: if power attached to some possible things but not others, that would lead either to the power's own origination (hadath) or to the conversion of a possible into an impossible. The explanation is that all possible things are alike in their possibility, which is the basis for being an object of power; differential treatment would bring about absurdity. The footnote adds that the relation of power to the necessary is creative, which entails temporality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's power not extending to the impossible is a sign of limitation.
Correction: Sanusi explains that it is not a deficiency because the impossible is not a genuine candidate for being effected; rather, power would be deficient if it tried to affect it.

Checkpoint
Why does God's power not attach to the necessary (wajib) according to Sanusi?
A) Because the necessary does not exist.
B) Because attaching to it would imply its existence after non-existence, contradicting its necessity.
C) Because God chooses not to.
D) Because the necessary is beyond power.

Key Takeaway
God's power is exclusively directed to possible things, and this does not indicate any imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible to resolve, do you truly believe that God's power is not limited by what you consider impossible, or do you secretly think some things are beyond even Him? How can you train your heart to trust that His power covers only what is possible, yet that includes every genuine possibility for your life?
?
Sanusi says that not being able to affect the impossible is not a deficiency in God. But have you ever felt disappointed with God when He did not bring about something you considered necessary? Could it be that your demand was actually impossible in His wisdom? Will you repent for expecting God to act against His own perfection?
?
If God's power only relates to possible things, then every blessing in your life was once a mere possibility that He chose to actualize. Do you live with gratitude for the countless possibilities He has brought into being for you, or do you take them for granted? Commit right now to thank Him for one specific possibility He turned into reality for you today.

Divine Attributes
advanced
7 min
Refuting the Mu'tazilites: All Possibles Are Under God's Power
Sanusi refutes Mu'tazilite view on human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof that God's power extends to all possible things, including human voluntary actions, and how he refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Denying God's power over some possibles leads to absurdity: either God's power is temporal or a possible becomes impossible.

After establishing that power relates only to possibles, Sanusi defends the universality of divine power. The Mu'tazilites claimed that human voluntary actions are created by humans, not by God. Sanusi provides a logical proof: if power were excluded from some possibles, it would imply either that power originated in time (hadath) or that a possible turned into an impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazilites argued that human voluntary actions are not under God's power; humans create their own actions. Sanusi refutes this.
This is a major theological dispute.
2
Read Sanusi's proof
Sanusi argues that if power attached to some possibles but not others, two absurdities follow: either power itself becomes originated (hadath) or a possible becomes impossible.
Focus on the logical entailment.
3
Analyze why similarity matters
Sanusi explains that all possibles are alike in their possibility, which is the ground for being an object of power. Differential treatment would be arbitrary and lead to contradiction.
This is key to the argument.
4
See the refutation of the Mu'tazilites
The conclusion is that God's power encompasses all possibles, including human actions, refuting the Mu'tazilite view.

Generality of His power's relation to every possible. He said: 'And it is necessary that this power be related to all possible things.' He means by possible things: the permissible, not the necessary nor the impossible. As for the necessary: such as His essence and His attributes. It is not related to the necessary because its relation to it would require that it exist after non-existence since that is a necessary consequence of the effect of power, and that would overturn the reality of the necessary, for it is that which cannot be conceived in the mind as non-existent. As for the impossible: such as combining existence and non-existence of a thing, for example. It is not related to it because its relation to it would require that it be sound for it to exist, and that too contradicts the reality of the impossible, for it is that whose existence cannot be conceived in the mind. So it is determined that power is only related to the permissible, which is the possible, because it is that whose existence and non-existence are sound in the mind. And it should not be imagined that in the non-relation of power to the necessary and the impossible there is deficiency; because deficiency is only established if the thing's reality were of the kind that accepts existence after non-existence, and then despite that it could not be an effect of power. But if the thing's reality is outside the genus of the powerful over, then there is no deficiency whatsoever in the non-relation of power to it. Rather, in its relation to it there would be a deficiency in power, or rather its non-existence altogether; because if it related to the necessary like the exalted essence, for example, the origination of the essence would be necessary, and its origination would prevent the existence of the power subsisting in it before it. And his saying: 'with all possible things' pointed by this to establishing the fourth point, which is the generality of power over all possible things, in refutation of the doctrine of the Mu'tazila who exclude the voluntary actions of servants from being powerful over by God Almighty, and they make the servants themselves the ones who invent them by their own will! And he argued in the creed for the doctrine of the people of truth: that if power were related to some possible things without others, there would follow from it either its origination or the conversion of the possible into impossible. And the explanation of the entailment: that possible things are similar in the possibility that renders them suitable (for being objects of power). (Footnote: It entailed origination only because the relation of power is a relation of effect; and there is no meaning to the relation of power to the necessary except bringing it into existence and supplying it with means of permanence, each of which necessitates origination. — Safaqisi)

Commentary
This passage is a detailed doctrinal argument about the universality of God's power (qudra). It begins by defining the objects of power: God's power relates only to possible things (mumkinat), which are the permissible (ja'izat), and not to the necessary (wajib) or impossible (mustahil). The author explains why: if power related to the necessary, it would imply that the necessary came into existence after non-existence, which contradicts its necessity. Similarly, relating to the impossible would require that the impossible be existent, which contradicts its impossibility. Therefore, power is confined to the possible, defined as what the mind can conceive as both existing and non-existing. The author then addresses a potential misunderstanding: that restricting power to the possible might seem like a deficiency. He argues that deficiency only occurs if a thing's nature makes it suitable for being an effect of power, but it is not. If a thing's reality is beyond the class of what can be acted upon, then not relating to it is not a deficiency; rather, relating to it would imply a deficiency or even non-existence of power. For example, if power related to the necessary, it would necessitate the origination of the essence, which would nullify the power subsisting in it. Next, the author turns to the fourth point, which is the universality of power over all possible things, in refutation of the Mu'tazilites who claimed that human voluntary actions are not within God's power but are created by humans themselves. He provides a proof: if power attached to some possible things but not others, that would lead either to the power's own origination (hadath) or to the conversion of a possible into an impossible. The explanation is that all possible things are alike in their possibility, which is the basis for being an object of power; differential treatment would bring about absurdity. The footnote adds that the relation of power to the necessary is creative, which entails temporality.

What does Sanusi argue would follow if power were related to some possible things but not others?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believe that every action—including your most private thoughts—is under God's power, what excuse remains for not relying on Him in every moment? Will you commit today to attributing all your successes and failures to His will, not your own?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that human responsibility requires independence from God's power. Do you ever act as if your choices are autonomous from God? How can Sanusi's logical proof that such independence leads to absurdity change your daily attitude of humility before Him?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
The Universality of Divine Power
Sanusi proves divine power extends to all possibles via a two-branch logical proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that divine power (qudra) must extend to all possible things, and why limiting it leads to logical absurdity.

Core Idea
Divine power cannot be limited; any restriction would make God temporal or turn possibles into impossibles.

Sanusi now tackles a critical point: does divine power cover everything possible, including human voluntary actions? The Mu'tazila argued that humans create their own acts, thereby limiting divine power over those acts. Sanusi provides a masterful rational proof that such limitation is impossible. Before presenting that proof, he first clarifies that power only attaches to what is possible, not to the necessary or impossible — this is not a deficiency but a matter of definition. Then he argues that if power were limited to only some possibles, it would lead to either God being temporal (if a specifier caused the limitation) or the possible becoming impossible (if no specifier). Both are absurd, so power must be universal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Notice that the passage has two main parts: a clarification about what power can attach to, and the main proof of universality.
Pay attention to the key terms: mumkin, wajib, mustahil, mukhaṣṣiṣ.
2
Understand the two-branch proof
The proof is a conditional argument: if power is limited to some possibles, then either there is a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) causing that limitation or not. Trace each branch and see why both lead to absurd conclusions.
The specifier branch leads to power being temporal; the no-specifier branch leads to the possible becoming impossible.
3
Reflect on the refutation of Mu'tazila
Consider how this proof refutes the Mu'tazila claim that human voluntary acts are outside divine power. If divine power were limited, the absurdities would follow, so it must extend to all possibles including human acts.
This argument establishes the fourth point of Sanusi's creed.

منافٍ لحقيقة المستحيل ؛ إذْ هو ما لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده فتعيَّنَ : أنَّ القدرة لا تتعلَّقُ إلا بالجائز ؛ وهو الممكن ؛ لأنَّهُ الذي يصحُ في العقلِ وجودُهُ وعدمُهُ ، ولا يُتوهّمُ أَنَّ في عدم تعلُّق القدرة بالواجب والمستحيل قصوراً ؛ لأنَّ القصورَ إِنَّما يثبتُ أَنْ لو كانَتْ حقيقة الشيء مما يقبلُ الوجود بعد العدم ، ثم معَ ذلك لم يتأتَ أنْ يكون أثراً للقدرةِ ، أمَّا إذا كانَتْ حقيقةُ الشيء خارجةً عن جنسِ المقدور .. فليسَ في عدم تعلَّقِ القدرةِ بهِ قصور ألبتة ، بل في تعلُّقها به يلزم قصورها ، بل عدمُها ألبتة ؛ لأنَّها لو تعلَّقتْ بالواجب كالذاتِ العلية مثلاً .. لزم حدوث الذاتِ ، وحدوثها يمنع وجود القدرة القائمة بها قبلها (١) وقوله : ( بجميعِ الممكناتِ ( أشار بهذا إلى إثباتِ المطلبِ الرابع ؛ وهو تعميم القدرة في جميع الممكنات ؛ ردّاً لمذهب المعتزلة الذين أخرجوا أفعال العبادِ الاختياريَّةَ عن أن تكون مقدورةً الله تعالى وجعلوا العبادَ هم الذين اخترعوها بإرادتهم ! و احتج في العقيدة لمذهب أهل الحقِّ : بأنَّ القدرة لو تعلَّقَتْ ببعض الممكناتِ دونَ بعض .. للزمَ عليهِ إمَّا حدوثها ، أو انقلاب الممكنِ مستحيلاً ، وبيانُ اللزوم : أنَّ الممكناتِ متماثلة في الإمكان المصححِ لتعلُّقِ القدرة ، فاختصاص بعضها بصلاحيته لتعلق القدرة دونَ الآخر : إنْ كانَ لمُخصِّص : لزمَ حدوث القدرةِ ؛ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ يُحتاجُ حينئذ إلى أنْ يكونَ الفاعل المخصّص خلق قدرةً تتعلق ببعض الممكنات ، وعجزاً يتعلَّق بالبعض الآخرِ ، وقد عرفت برهان وجوبِ القدم لقدرته تعالى وإنْ كانَ تخصيص تعلُّقها بالبعض لا لمُخصّص : لزمَ أَنَّ البعض الآخر لا يصلح لذاتِهِ أنْ يكونَ مُتعلَّقاً لها ؛ لما فُرِضَ مِنْ عدمِ المخصص ، وما لا يصلحُ لذاتِهِ أنْ يكونَ مُتعلقاً للقدرة ينحصر في الواجب والمستحيل ، وكونُ هذا البعض الممكن واجباً لا يصح ؛ لأنَّه معدوم لم يُوجَدْ ، ولا شيء مِنَ الواجب بمعدوم ، فتعيَّنَ أَنْ يكونَ مستحيلاً ، والفَرْضُ أنَّه ممكن مماثل لسائر الممكنات التي تعلقت بها القدرة ، فقد لزم انقلابُ الممكن مستحيلاً ، وإذا انقلب هذا الممكنُ مستحيلاً لزم انقلاب سائرِ الممكناتِ مستحيلاً ؛ للتماثل ، فلا يَقدِرُ الإله على شيء منها ، ويلزمُ ألا يُوجَدَ شيء منها ، والعقلُ والعِيانُ يُكذِّبُ ذلك ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق
Contradictory to the reality of the impossible; for it is that whose existence is inconceivable in the mind. So it is determined that power is not attached except to the permissible (al-ja'iz), which is the possible (al-mumkin), because it is that whose existence and non-existence are valid in the mind. And it should not be imagined that in the non-attachment of power to the necessary (al-wajib) and the impossible (al-mustahil) is deficiency; because deficiency is only established if the reality of a thing were such as to admit existence after non-existence, and despite that it could not be an effect of power. But if the reality of a thing is outside the genus of the power-able (al-maqdur), then there is no deficiency at all in the non-attachment of power to it. Rather, in its attachment to it would entail its deficiency, indeed its non-existence entirely; because if it were attached to the necessary, like the eternal Essence for example, it would entail the temporality of the Essence, and its temporality prevents the existence of the power subsisting in it before it. And his saying: “with all possibles (bī-jamīʿ al-mumkināt)” — he indicated by this the establishment of the fourth point, which is the generalization of power over all possibles, in refutation of the school of the Muʿtazila, who removed the voluntary actions of servants from being power-able for God Most High, and made the servants themselves the inventors of them by their own will! And he argued in al-ʿAqīda for the school of the people of truth: that if power were attached to some possibles without others, it would necessarily entail either its temporality or the transformation of the possible into the impossible. And the explanation of the entailment: that possibles are similar in the possibility (al-imkān) that qualifies for the attachment of power. So the specification of some of them as suitable for the attachment of power to the exclusion of the others: if it is due to a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ), then the temporality of power is entailed, in that then there would be need for the specifier agent to create a power that attaches to some possibles, and an incapacity that attaches to the others — and you already know the proof of the necessity of pre-eternity for His power Most High. And if the specification of its attachment to some is not due to a specifier, then it is entailed that the others are not in themselves suitable to be attached to it, given the assumption of the absence of a specifier; and what is not in itself suitable to be an attachment of power is limited to the necessary and the impossible. And that this particular possible should be necessary is not valid, because it is non-existent, not brought into existence, and nothing of the necessary is non-existent. So it is determined that it must be impossible; but the assumption is that it is a possible similar to the rest of the possibles to which power is attached. Thus the transformation of the possible into the impossible is entailed. And if this possible is transformed into the impossible, the transformation of all other possibles into the impossible is entailed, due to similarity. So God would have no power over any of them, and it would entail that nothing of them would exist; but reason and observation belie that. And with God Most High is [granting] success.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. It concludes an earlier argument about why divine power (qudra) does not attach to the impossible or the necessary, then introduces and proves the fourth point of Sanusi's creed: that divine power extends to all possible things, including human voluntary acts, against the Mu'tazila. The author first explains that power only attaches to possible (mumkin) things, not to the necessary (wajib) or impossible (mustahil). He argues that this is not a deficiency in power because deficiency only arises when a thing could in principle be produced but is not; but the necessary and impossible are not of the genus of things that can be produced. He then quotes Sanusi's phrase "with all possibles" to introduce the fourth point: the generalization of power over all possibles, refuting the Mu'tazila who exclude human voluntary acts from divine power. The central proof is a two-branched conditional argument: if divine power were attached to some possibles but not others, then either there is a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) for that limitation or not. If there is a specifier, then divine power would be temporal (ḥādith) because the specifier would have to create both a power for some things and an incapacity for others — but God's power is necessarily eternal. If there is no specifier, then the excluded possibles would be unsuitable by themselves for power; but only the necessary and impossible are inherently unsuitable. Since they are assumed possible but non-existent, they cannot be necessary; so they must be impossible. But they are assumed possible, so this would mean a possible becomes impossible — which is absurd and would lead to all possibles being impossible, contradicting reason and observation. Thus the original premise (that power is limited) is false, and power must extend to all possibles.
